            <meta name="version-id" content="27060">
            <meta name="article-id" content="24532">
            <meta name="dc.title" content="Occupational structure of bearers of Jewish rabbinical, occupational and generic surnames">
            <meta name="dc.description" content="We study choice of profession in three groups of Russian-speaking Jewish families with different occupational distributions of the ancestors. This study continues exploration of the persistence of social status of families over centuries that was initiated in recent years. It was found previously that in some cases professions remain associated with the same surnames for many generations. Here the studied groups are defined by a&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;of the&amp;nbsp;surname of individuals composing them. The&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;serves as a label that indicates a professional bias of the ancestors of the individual.&amp;nbsp;One group are the bearers of the class of surnames which were used by rabbinical dynasties. The other group is constituted by occupational surnames, mostly connected to crafts. Finally, the last group are generic Jewish names defined as surnames belonging to neither of the above groups. We use the database that consists of 858 and 1057 of the first two groups, respectively, and 7471 generic Jewish surnames. The statistics of the database are those of individuals drawn at random from the considered groups. We determine shares of members of the groups working in a given type of occupations together with the confidence interval. The occupational type&rsquo;s definition agrees with International Standard Classification of Occupations. It is demonstrated that there is a statistically significant difference in the occupational structure of the three groups that holds beyond the uncertainty allowed by 95% confidence interval. We quantify the difference with a numerical measure of the overlap of professional preferences of different groups. We conclude that in our study the occupational bias of different population groups is preserved at least for two centuries that passed since the considered surnames appeared.">

            <meta name="citation_title" content="Occupational structure of bearers of Jewish rabbinical, occupational and generic surnames">
            <meta name="citation_abstract" content="We study choice of profession in three groups of Russian-speaking Jewish families with different occupational distributions of the ancestors. This study continues exploration of the persistence of social status of families over centuries that was initiated in recent years. It was found previously that in some cases professions remain associated with the same surnames for many generations. Here the studied groups are defined by a&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;of the&amp;nbsp;surname of individuals composing them. The&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;serves as a label that indicates a professional bias of the ancestors of the individual.&amp;nbsp;One group are the bearers of the class of surnames which were used by rabbinical dynasties. The other group is constituted by occupational surnames, mostly connected to crafts. Finally, the last group are generic Jewish names defined as surnames belonging to neither of the above groups. We use the database that consists of 858 and 1057 of the first two groups, respectively, and 7471 generic Jewish surnames. The statistics of the database are those of individuals drawn at random from the considered groups. We determine shares of members of the groups working in a given type of occupations together with the confidence interval. The occupational type&rsquo;s definition agrees with International Standard Classification of Occupations. It is demonstrated that there is a statistically significant difference in the occupational structure of the three groups that holds beyond the uncertainty allowed by 95% confidence interval. We quantify the difference with a numerical measure of the overlap of professional preferences of different groups. We conclude that in our study the occupational bias of different population groups is preserved at least for two centuries that passed since the considered surnames appeared.">
            <meta name="citation_description" content="We study choice of profession in three groups of Russian-speaking Jewish families with different occupational distributions of the ancestors. This study continues exploration of the persistence of social status of families over centuries that was initiated in recent years. It was found previously that in some cases professions remain associated with the same surnames for many generations. Here the studied groups are defined by a&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;of the&amp;nbsp;surname of individuals composing them. The&amp;nbsp;class&amp;nbsp;serves as a label that indicates a professional bias of the ancestors of the individual.&amp;nbsp;One group are the bearers of the class of surnames which were used by rabbinical dynasties. The other group is constituted by occupational surnames, mostly connected to crafts. Finally, the last group are generic Jewish names defined as surnames belonging to neither of the above groups. We use the database that consists of 858 and 1057 of the first two groups, respectively, and 7471 generic Jewish surnames. The statistics of the database are those of individuals drawn at random from the considered groups. We determine shares of members of the groups working in a given type of occupations together with the confidence interval. The occupational type&rsquo;s definition agrees with International Standard Classification of Occupations. It is demonstrated that there is a statistically significant difference in the occupational structure of the three groups that holds beyond the uncertainty allowed by 95% confidence interval. We quantify the difference with a numerical measure of the overlap of professional preferences of different groups. We conclude that in our study the occupational bias of different population groups is preserved at least for two centuries that passed since the considered surnames appeared.">

{
  "@context": "https://schema.org",
  "@type": "ScholarlyArticle",
  "mainEntityOfPage": {
    "@type": "WebPage",
    "@id": "https://f1000research.com/articles/9-971"
  },
  "headline": "Occupational structure of bearers of Jewish rabbinical, occupational and generic surnames",
  "datePublished": "2020-08-11T13:01:52",
  "dateModified": "2020-08-11T13:01:52",
  "author": [
    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Alexander Jonathan Vidgop"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Nelly Norton"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Nechama Rosenberg"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Malka Haguel-Spitzberg"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Itzhak Fouxon"
    }  ],
  "publisher": {
    "@type": "Organization",
    "name": "F1000Research",
    "logo": {
      "@type": "ImageObject",
      "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
      "height": 480,
      "width": 60
    }
  },
  "image": {
    "@type": "ImageObject",
    "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
    "height": 1200,
    "width": 150
  },
  "description": "We study choice of profession in three groups of Russian-speaking Jewish families with different occupational distributions of the ancestors. This study continues exploration of the persistence of social status of families over centuries that was initiated in recent years. It was found previously that in some cases professions remain associated with the same surnames for many generations. Here the studied groups are defined by a class of the surname of individuals composing them. The class serves as a label that indicates a professional bias of the ancestors of the individual. One group are the bearers of the class of surnames which were used by rabbinical dynasties. The other group is constituted by occupational surnames, mostly connected to crafts. Finally, the last group are generic Jewish names defined as surnames belonging to neither of the above groups. We use the database that consists of 858 and 1057 of the first two groups, respectively, and 7471 generic Jewish surnames. The statistics of the database are those of individuals drawn at random from the considered groups. We determine shares of members of the groups working in a given type of occupations together with the confidence interval. The occupational type&rsquo;s definition agrees with International Standard Classification of Occupations. It is demonstrated that there is a statistically significant difference in the occupational structure of the three groups that holds beyond the uncertainty allowed by 95% confidence interval. We quantify the difference with a numerical measure of the overlap of professional preferences of different groups. We conclude that in our study the occupational bias of different population groups is preserved at least for two centuries that passed since the considered surnames appeared."
}

class=status-icons> <em>AWAITING PEER REVIEW</em> </span> </div> </div> </div> </div> <h2 class="article-headings article-page-abstract" id=anchor-abstract> <span class="f1r-article-mobile-inline abstract-heading-border"></span> Abstract </h2> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout"> <div class="abstract-text is-expanded"> We study choice of profession in three groups of Russian-speaking Jewish families with different occupational distributions of the ancestors. This study continues exploration of the persistence of social status of families over centuries that was initiated in recent years. It was found previously that in some cases professions remain associated with the same surnames for many generations. Here the studied groups are defined by a&nbsp;class&nbsp;of the&nbsp;surname of individuals composing them. The&nbsp;class&nbsp;serves as a label that indicates a professional bias of the ancestors of the individual.&nbsp;One group are the bearers of the class of surnames which were used by rabbinical dynasties. The other group is constituted by occupational surnames, mostly connected to crafts. Finally, the last group are generic Jewish names defined as surnames belonging to neither of the above groups. We use the database that consists of 858 and 1057 of the first two groups, respectively, and 7471 generic Jewish surnames. The statistics of the database are those of individuals drawn at random from the considered groups. We determine shares of members of the groups working in a given type of occupations together with the confidence interval. The occupational types definition agrees with International Standard Classification of Occupations. It is demonstrated that there is a statistically significant difference in the occupational structure of the three groups that holds beyond the uncertainty allowed by 95% confidence interval. We quantify the difference with a numerical measure of the overlap of professional preferences of different groups. We conclude that in our study the occupational bias of different population groups is preserved at least for two centuries that passed since the considered surnames appeared. </div> <div <div class="popup-window corresponding-authors-popup"> <div class=corresponding-author-container> <div class="popup-window-title small">Corresponding Author(s)</div> <div class=authors> Itzhak Fouxon (<a href="mailto:itzhak8@gmail.com">itzhak8@gmail.com</a>) </div> </div> <div class="margin-top margin-bottom float-left"> <button id=close-popup-window class=general-white-orange-button>Close</button> </div> </div> </div> </div> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-corresponding-author> <b>Corresponding author:</b> Itzhak Fouxon </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom competing-interests-display"> <span class=competing-interests-title>Competing interests:</span> No competing grants were involved in supporting this work. </div> <span class="f1r-article-desk info-separation padding-bottom"> <span class="copywrite-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-100_open_access"></span> </span> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-copyright-text> <b>Copyright:</b>&nbsp;  2020 Vidgop AJ <em>et al</em>. This is an open access article distributed under the terms of the <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" target=_blank data-test-id=box-licence-link>Creative Commons Attribution License</a>, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited. </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom" data-test-id=box-how-to-cite> <b>How to cite:</b> <span class="article-title-and-info in-article-box"> Vidgop AJ, Norton N, Rosenberg N <em>et al.</em> Occupational structure of bearers of Jewish rabbinical, occupational and generic surnames [version 1; peer review: awaiting peer review]</span>. <i>F1000Research</i> 2020, <b>9</b>:971 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1</a>) </span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-first-published><b>First published:</b> 11 Aug 2020, <b>9</b>:971 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1</a>)</span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-latest-published><b>Latest published:</b> 11 Aug 2020, <b>9</b>:971 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24532.1</a>)</span> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div id=article-context class=article-context> <div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d5710e194>Introduction</h2><p class="" id=d5710e197>Recently usage of surnames in studies of intergenerational mobility, such as investigations of temporal changes of representations of different surnames in various social groups, has developed into an established tool of research (see e.g. <a href="#ref-3">Clark, 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-4">Clark, 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-5">Clark <i>et al.,</i> 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-7">Gell <i>et al.,</i> 2015</a> and references therein; <a href="#ref-8">Santavirta &amp; Stuhler, 2019</a> for a recent review). In a typical study, the frequency of occurrence of certain surnames in different professional or elite or other population groups is considered. This frequency is compared with the frequency of the surnames occurrence in the general population. If it is found that the surname occurs in some group significantly more than in the general population, then the surname is overrepresented in the group. Conversely, if the surname occurs less, then it is underrepresented.</p><p class="" id=d5710e221>Probably the main result of the aforementioned extensive studies is that the over- or under-representations do not change over long periods of time, much longer than would be implied by the conventional mobility measures. Those measures average over society, thus hiding the underlying low mobility rates for a given surname. These studies have been performed for different countries and cultures (see <a href="#ref-3">Clark, 2015</a> and references therein).</p><p class="" id=d5710e227>In this work, we perform a study for a population of Russian-speaking Jews who have not been so far considered in this type of study. We investigate occupational distribution of three different groups in the population that are defined by different biases in the occupational distribution of their ancestors. The size of 9315 individuals of the studied pool of data allows us to derive rigorous statistics of the groups (we use here rounding, explained below). We demonstrate that the distributions are different beyond the uncertainty allowed by the confidence interval. This finding shows that biases in occupational distributions can be preserved for at least two centuries that passed, since all Jews have had an inherited surname. This provides yet another demonstration of comparatively slow surnames mobility, defined as social mobility of individuals with a given surname, (<a href="#ref-3">Clark, 2015</a>). Our results also provide the occupational distribution of the Russian speaking Jewry of the twentieth century, a result that has its own interest.</p><p class="" id=d5710e233>The objectives of our study are quite similar to those of <a href="#ref-4">Clark (2012)</a>, where the statistics of several groups of surnames in Sweden are considered. <a href="#ref-4">Clark (2012)</a> considered noble surnames, names that were once given to nobility; Latinized surnames were adopted by the educated class and certain other groups. Similarly, we consider rabbinical surnames (counterpart of nobility), surnames of craftsmen (hereafter called occupational), and others that fall outside of these categories (generic).</p><p class="" id=d5710e243>Rabbinical surnames are those whose first bearer was a rabbi. Rabbis constituted the elite of their time, the most respected class of the Jewish population, and they can be considered as a kind of the Jewish nobility. The rabbi of the surnames origin could be a prominent figure living many centuries ago, for example as in the case of Luria, Shapiro or Halperin. Family names of these dynasties were often taken as a sign of distinction. However, other rabbinical families are only two hundred years old, for example Rabinovich. For these clans, in the beginning of their history, the profession of the rabbi was often passed from father to son for a number of generations. Occupational surnames derive from the name of the craft of the first bearer of the name. Craftsmen had the knowledge of their craft and present a rough counterpart to the educated class considered by <a href="#ref-4">Clark (2012)</a>, for example Schuster (shoemaker), Mednik (tinker) and Portnoi (tailor). In these families the professions were also often passed between generations. Finally, generic surnames consist mostly of surnames whose origin has nothing to do with the professions of their founder.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d5710e252>Methods</h2><div class=section><a name=d5710e255 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Participants</h3><p class="" id=d5710e260>Our data was acquired over four years (November 2015 - February 2020) from individuals who were part of an educational family history program that was implemented by the Am haZikaron Institute for Science and Heritage of the Jewish People in Tel Aviv, Israel.</p><p class="" id=d5710e263>The individuals were Russian-speaking Jewish family members residing in the Former Soviet Union (FSU). They voluntarily provided genealogical data for the program via online forms that were sent to them before their arrival in Tel Aviv (see section Data collection). The forms were presented in the native language of the individual (Russian) and informed the individuals that their data could be used for future academic purposes. Completion of the form was taken as consent to allow their data to be used for this academic study (some participants chose not to complete the form). This study did not seek ethical approval as it was deemed low risk, none of the participants were considered vulnerable, the participants consented for their data to be used in future academic research, and all participants were over 18 years of age.</p><p class="" id=d5710e266>Data from an individual was not selected for inclusion in this study if it was intentionally distorted. Data verification was accomplished during a meeting in Tel Aviv between the authors.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d5710e270 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Data collection</h3><p class="" id=d5710e275>Information was obtained on professions of a participants family members for the last four generations. This resulted in a collection of data on individuals who were born throughout the 20th century. The educational programs at Am haZikaron are open to all, and so there was no known statistical bias toward any particular professions. Similarly, there is no known correlation between the starting letter of the name and the profession. Hence, to the best of our judgement, the obtained data, arranged alphabetically, is a random list of Jewish individuals and their professions. The randomness holds up to small clusters of individuals who belong to the same family and have some correlations. These correlations are yet to be studied and are not the focus of our study. It will be seen later that randomness is consistent with the statistics.</p><p class="" id=d5710e278>In the research by Clark <i>et al</i> (<a href="#ref-3">Clark, 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-4">Clark, 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-5">Clark <i>et al.,</i> 2015</a>), the major source of information were professional directories that list all individuals in a particular professional area. In contrast, our data is a random pool of the population that necessitates a different methodology for analysis. We partitioned the population into the three groups of surnames (rabbinical, occupational, generic), which could be biased with respect to their occupational distribution due to the bias in their ancestry. We checked if the bias persists through time and found that there is a statistically significant difference in the professional preferences of the three groups.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d5710e297 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Dataset description and considerations</h3><p class="" id=d5710e302>We collected the data over four years until the pool included a statistically significant amount of surnames where the confidence interval allowed to reasonably fix the share of each profession in the total population. We obtained data on 858 (57.8.% men) bearers of rabbinical and 1057 (59.7% men) bearers of occupational surnames. The other 7471 (57.6% men) individuals had a generic surname, which was neither rabbinical not occupational. Men are slightly overrepresented since the maiden names of the female family members were sometimes unknown to the participants. This slight difference in gender composition of the groups may cause some professional bias; however, this is negligible compared with the magnitude of the groups differences (shown below).</p><p class="" id=d5710e305>The studied population included different birth cohorts. The earliest birth date for an individual in the data was 1858 and the latest 2001. For adequate comparison of the groups, we must have roughly the same share of each group born in each of the considered generations. Therefore, we divided the historical period spanned by our data into four periods (1858-1894, 1895-1930, 1931-1966, 1967-2001; <a href="#T1">Table 1</a>).</p><a name=T1 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 1. Data description showing the percentage of types of surname according to four generations considered.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d5710e322 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d5710e324 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d5710e326 class=n-a></a><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=2 valign=middle><a name=d5710e328 class=n-a></a>Generation born</th><th name=d5710e367 class=n-a></a>18951930</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e370 class=n-a></a>44.8</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e373 class=n-a></a>35.6</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e391 class=n-a></a>38.0</td></tr><tr><a name=d5710e395 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e397 class=n-a></a>19672001</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e400 class=n-a></a>15.8</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e403 class=n-a></a>15.8</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e406 class=n-a></a>16.1</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d5710e413>From <a href="#T1">Table 1</a>, it is seen that the birth date distributions of the groups are very similar so that the comparison of occupations is reasonable. The difference of shares of different generations holds for many reasons: each participant was asked to fill the data for two parents, four grandparents and eight great grandparents, where the data on the older generations was often forgotten, while the younger generation could still be studying or have no profession yet. However, the precise form of the birth date distribution is irrelevant for our comparative study, for only birth date distributions of different groups are similar.</p><p class="" id=d5710e420>The birthplaces were scattered all over the territory of the FSU. Jewish families have a long tradition of studying and those who would want to acquire education would typically receive such an opportunity. In other words, with a good approximation, an individual born into a Jewish family of the FSU would have an equal opportunity for getting that or another profession irrespective of birthplace. Therefore, we disregarded the geographical factor in our study.</p><p class="" id=d5710e423>No detectable bias toward some profession due to a different number of reported family members was observed. This number was never too large, and rarely reached five individuals (other ancestors were not Jewish and not considered by our study).</p><p class="" id=d5710e426>The data on professions was self-reported in the native (Russian) language of the participant and was not standardized. We processed the data to a standard of occupations according to the methodology below.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d5710e430 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Data analysis</h3><p class="" id=d5710e435><i><b>Grouping the data by profession.</b></i> We separated the data into the three groups described (rabbinical, occupational, generic). We then grouped the professions into 23 narrower professional activities. These were defined either by their significant presence in the data, e.g. bookkeepers who constituted about 5% of all individuals, or by a unique character of the profession, for example interpreter/linguist. The groupings of professions, when performed, did not contradict the <a target=xrefwindow href="https://www.ilo.org/public/english/bureau/stat/isco/" id=d5710e440>International Standard Classification of Occupations</a>. The 23 categories of professions were as follows:</p><div class=list><a name=d5710e444 class=n-a></a><ul style="list-style-type: none"><li><p id=d5710e451><span class=label>1. </span> Engineer  by far the largest fraction of the studied population</p></li><li><p id=d5710e460><span class=label>2. </span> Physician</p></li><li><p id=d5710e469><span class=label>3. </span> Teacher</p></li><li><p id=d5710e478><span class=label>4. </span> Bookkeeper</p></li><li><p id=d5710e487><span class=label>5. </span> Worker</p></li><li><p id=d5710e497><span class=label>6. </span> Creative profession</p></li><li><p id=d5710e506><span class=label>7. </span> Economist</p></li><li><p id=d5710e515><span class=label>8. </span> Head/chief officer </p></li><li><p id=d5710e524><span class=label>9. </span> Nurse</p></li><li><p id=d5710e533><span class=label>10. </span> Researcher</p></li><li><p id=d5710e542><span class=label>11. </span> Clerical worker</p></li><li><p id=d5710e552><span class=label>12. </span> Armed forces</p></li><li><p id=d5710e561><span class=label>13. </span> Programmer</p></li><li><p id=d5710e570><span class=label>14. </span> Salesmen</p></li><li><p id=d5710e579><span class=label>15. </span> Businessman</p></li><li><p id=d5710e588><span class=label>16. </span> Legal professional</p></li><li><p id=d5710e597><span class=label>17. </span> Driver</p></li><li><p id=d5710e607><span class=label>18. </span> Interpreter, linguist</p></li><li><p id=d5710e616><span class=label>19. </span> Literary worker</p></li><li><p id=d5710e625><span class=label>20. </span> Pharmacist</p></li><li><p id=d5710e634><span class=label>21. </span> Librarian</p></li><li><p id=d5710e643><span class=label>22. </span> Psychologist</p></li><li><p id=d5710e652><span class=label>23. </span> Rarely occurring profession (other)</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d5710e657>We calculated the number of individuals having each one of the above professions for each of the three studied groups of surnames. The main target of our study is the share of each profession (P<sub>i</sub>) in each of the considered three groups of surnames. Thus, if N is the total number of members of Russian-speaking Jewish families with a generic surname then P<sub>i</sub>*N is the total number of members of these families with the i-th profession. For instance, P<sub>21</sub>*N would be the total number of librarians. The data on the full group consisting of millions of people (as defined by the fraction of Russian-speaking Jews whose names are neither rabbinical nor occupational where we count not only our contemporaries but all those who lived in the twentieth century) are unavailable. Thus, we have the standard problem of constraining P<sub>i</sub> from the incomplete information on the studied groups that is at our disposal. This is done by the statistical analysis relying on the observation that with good approximation our data constitute a random pool of the considered population groups.</p><p class="" id=d5710e672>A typical result of counting the professions is presented in <a href="#T2">Table 2</a> where the example of generic surnames is used. The total pool of data consisted of 7471 individuals. Due to presence of correlated clusters of individuals in the data, we found it instructive to use coarse-grained variables X<sub>i</sub>(k) for the statistical calculations. These variables separate the data into blocks of hundreds. For the pool size of 100, the statistical properties are seen already. Frequencies of different professions in each block of 100 are similar with some fluctuations. The usage of the variables X<sub>i</sub>(k) is necessary for the statistical considerations explained in detail below, otherwise they give an idea how the described laws apply in practice when the sample sizes are moderate. The statistics of X<sub>i</sub>(k) answers the question  if we took a pool of 100 representatives of one of the groups what would be the typical occurrences of each profession? </p><a name=T2 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 2. Results of counting the number of individuals with different professions by hundreds for the pool of 7471 bearers of generic surnames.</h3><p id=d5710e698>The first column provides the profession. The entries of the first row provide the considered range of numbers of people in the list considered in the corresponding column. For instance, the second column describes occupational distribution of 100 individuals with numbers from 1 to 100, the third column describes 100 individuals with numbers from 101 to 200 and so forth. Thus X<sub>7</sub>(300) is the number of individuals with the profession of economist in the range from 201 to 300. This number is located at the intersection of eighth row and fourth column, X<sub>7</sub>(300)=2  our list contains two economists in the portion of the list defined by 201300 range.</p></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d5710e708 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d5710e710 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d5710e712 class=n-a></a><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e714 class=n-a></a>Occupations</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e717 class=n-a></a>1100</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e720 class=n-a></a>101200</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e723 class=n-a></a>201300</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e726 class=n-a></a>301400</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e730 class=n-a></a>401500</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e733 class=n-a></a>501600</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e736 class=n-a></a>601700</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e739 class=n-a></a>701800</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e742 class=n-a></a>801900</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e745 class=n-a></a>9011000</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e749 class=n-a></a>Mean, <br class=br>7471</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d5710e756 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d5710e758 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e760 class=n-a></a>1. Literary worker</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1504 class=n-a></a>2</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1507 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1510 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1513 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1517 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1520 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1523 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1526 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1529 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1532 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1536 class=n-a></a>0.6</td></tr><tr><a name=d5710e1540 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1542 class=n-a></a>20. Pharmacist </td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1545 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1548 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1551 class=n-a></a>3</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1554 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1558 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1561 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1564 class=n-a></a>4</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1567 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1570 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1573 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1577 class=n-a></a>0.9</td></tr><tr><a name=d5710e1581 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1583 class=n-a></a>21. Librarian</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1586 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1589 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1592 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1595 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1599 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1602 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1605 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1608 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1611 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1614 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1618 class=n-a></a>0.4</td></tr><tr><a name=d5710e1622 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1624 class=n-a></a>22. Psychologist</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1627 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1630 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1633 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1636 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1640 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1643 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1646 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1649 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1652 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1655 class=n-a></a>2</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1659 class=n-a></a>0.5</td></tr><tr><a name=d5710e1663 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1665 class=n-a></a>23. Other</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1668 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1671 class=n-a></a>2</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1674 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1677 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1681 class=n-a></a>3</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1684 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1687 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1690 class=n-a></a>2</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1693 class=n-a></a>1</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1696 class=n-a></a>0</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e1700 class=n-a></a>0.9</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d5710e1707>Thus we took out of our pool the first 100 individuals and determined the numbers X<sub>i</sub>(100) of individuals with the i-th profession. Then we took 100 more individuals and determined the numbers X<sub>i</sub>(200) of individuals with the i-th profession in the range of 101200. Continuing in this way, we determined X<sub>i</sub>(k) determined by the columns in <a href="#T2">Table 2</a>. We used the pool size up to 1000 because this size allows comparison with the groups of rabbinical and occupational surnames where the total pool was about 1000. Thus, we used the pool of 7471 as the control size that allows to test how well the pool of 1000 individuals represents the whole considered group. Average over a limited pool size k gives an approximation p<sub>i</sub>(k) for P<sub>i</sub> defined above. Up to fluctuations p<sub>i</sub>(k) monotonously approach P<sub>i</sub> on increasing k and we can hope that a reasonable approximation to P<sub>i</sub> can be obtained already from the largest k available from our data. Indeed, we demonstrate quantitatively that distributions derived from the pools of 1000 and 7471 individuals are rather similar. Thus, making the reasonable assumption that pool size of 7471 represents the full group accurately, which is proved by the calculation of confidence intervals, we conclude that the occupational distribution of the generic surnames can be derived quite accurately (quantified below) from the distribution of 1000 individuals. Assuming that the groups of bearers of rabbinical and occupational surnames are similar statistically we can then conclude that the distributions for these groups, derived from the study of about 1000 individuals, provide a good characterization of the full groups. Despite that, we rely in our conclusions on the rigorously defined confidence intervals; qualitatively it is probable that the means obtained from studies of 858 rabbinical and 1057 occupational surnames provide accurate idea of the full groups.</p><p class="" id=d5710e1738><b><i>Distance between the distributions.</i></b> There was a need for quantitative comparison of distributions P<sub>i</sub> of the three considered groups (<a href="#f1">Figure 1</a>). Thus, considering the finite pool sizes approximations to P<sub>i</sub> in <a href="#f1">Figure 1</a> (in %), it is seen that they are different; however how different? To make the comparison, we calculated the distance between the distributions. There is no unique conventional definition of the distance between probability distributions. We used the Hellinger distance (see for example. <a href="#ref-9">Yang &amp; Le Cam, 2000</a>), whose definition can be understood by observing that since the distributions are normalized, <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M1 overflow=scroll><mrow><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mn>23</mn></mrow></msubsup><mrow></mrow></mstyle><msub><mtext>P</mtext><mi>i</mi></msub><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></span> then what needs the comparison are the shapes of the distributions. For instance, for all three distributions in <a href="#f1">Figure 1</a>, the heights of all bars sum to 100 and only the shapes distinguish the distributions. The shapes can be compared by considering the overlap, which is conveniently defined by the Bhattacharyya coefficient (<a href="#ref-2">Bhattacharyya, 1943</a>):</p><a name=f1 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/27060/16706ad6-f766-4bff-b5cd-316105160135_figure1.gif"><img alt="16706ad6-f766-4bff-b5cd-316105160135_figure1.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/27060/16706ad6-f766-4bff-b5cd-316105160135_figure1.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 1. Occupational distributions of the three groups of surnames according to the 23 professional activities (%).</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d5710e1811><a name=e1 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math1 overflow=scroll><mrow><mtext>BC</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>k</mtext><mo>,</mo><mspace width="0.4em"></mspace><mtext>p</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo></mo><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>i</mtext><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mn>23</mn></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>k</mtext></msubsup><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>p</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt><mo>,</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></div> </p><p class="" id=d5710e1865>where k and p are indices of the considered two groups (in this equation and below); when there is a need to indicate to which group P<sub>i</sub> refers, we use the notation <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M2 overflow=scroll><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>r</mtext></msubsup><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></span> <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M3 overflow=scroll><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>o</mtext></msubsup><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></span> <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M4 overflow=scroll><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>g</mtext></msubsup><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></span> for the P<sub>i</sub> of rabbinical, occupational and generic surnames, respectively. The Bhattacharyya coefficient is the scalar product (type of overlap) of two vectors in 23-dimensional space with components <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M5 overflow=scroll><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>k</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></span> and <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M6 overflow=scroll><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>p</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></span>. The square root is introduced in the definition because <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M7 overflow=scroll><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>k</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></span> are unit vectors in the 23-dimensional space, which allows definition of the shape of the distribution as the direction of these unit vectors. The coefficient changes between zero, holding for non-overlapping distributions, and one, holding for identical distributions. The Hellinger distance between the distributions is then defined as:</p><p class="" id=d5710e1954><a name=e2 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math2 overflow=scroll><mrow><mtext>H</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>k</mtext><mo>,</mo><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>p</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo></mo><msqrt><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo></mo><mtext>BC</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>k</mtext><mo>,</mo><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>p</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow></msqrt><mo>=</mo><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mrow><msqrt><mn>2</mn></msqrt></mrow></mrow><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mn>23</mn></mrow></msubsup><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>k</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt><mo>-</mo><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>p</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></mstyle><msup><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>.</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e2054>Thus, H(k, p) is proportional to the Euclidean distance between two vectors <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M8 overflow=scroll><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>k</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></span> and <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M9 overflow=scroll><mrow><msqrt><mrow><msubsup><mtext>P</mtext><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>p</mtext></msubsup></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></span> in the 23 dimensional spaces. It provides a good definition of the distance because the Euclidean distance does.</p><p class="" id=d5710e2083><i>Confidence interval under the random sampling assumption</i></p><p class="" id=d5710e2087>Our sample consists of 858 bearers of rabbinical surnames, 1057 bearers of occupational surnames and 7471 bearers of generic surnames (we often use in the calculations the rounded number of 7400). These samples are quite large; however, the population means that can be derived from them still contain quite a large uncertainty. Here we describe the derivation of the confidence interval, i.e. the interval within which the population means are contained with high probability (95% probability in our study). The derivation in this section is done assuming that our list of individuals is a random sample of the studied population groups. This is a good assumption up to the presence of sequences of 35 individuals with the same surname who belong to the same close family. These individuals can be assumed to have a certain correlation of professions, which violates the assumption of random sampling. The consistency of the assumption of random sampling despite these correlations will be demonstrated in the next section.</p><p class="" id=d5710e2090>The material in the rest of this section is mostly well-known. We consider a total population of X individuals, of whom X<sub>i</sub> have a property i. In the application of interest in this work, this property is a certain profession; however, the nature of this property is irrelevant for general considerations. We make a random sampling of the population, i.e. we pick an individual at random. Then, by definition of random sampling, the individual with property i is picked with probability p<sub>i</sub> = X<sub>i</sub>/X. If we continue the random sampling, then the probability distribution of the number X<sub>i</sub>(N) of individuals with the property i in randomly picked N individuals is given by the binomial distribution with the success probability p<sub>i</sub>. Here we assume that N is much smaller than both X<sub>i</sub> and X so that the random sampling occurs in approximately identical conditions. The average and variance of X<sub>i</sub>(N) are given by the well-known formulas of binomial distribution</p><p class="" id=d5710e2115><a name=e3 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math3 overflow=scroll><mrow><mo>&lt;</mo><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>&gt;</mo><mo>=</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>N</mtext><mo>,</mo><mspace width="0.40em"></mspace><mo>&lt;</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>-</mo><mo>&lt;</mo><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>&gt;</mo><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>&gt;</mo><mo>=</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>-</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>,</mo><mspace width=8em></mspace><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e2224>where here and below the angular brackets stand for averaging. Large N binomial distribution can be approximated by Gaussian, implying that the distribution of X<sub>i</sub>(N) is Gaussian and is determined uniquely by the mean and the variance above. We find that the distribution of x(X<sub>i</sub>(N)- p<sub>i</sub> N)/[p<sub>i</sub> N(1- p<sub>i</sub>)]<sup>1/2</sup> is the standard normal distribution with zero mean and unit variance. For this distribution, it is well-known that the probability that x will fall between -1.96 and 1.96 is approximately 0.95. This probability equals the probability that X<sub>i</sub>(N)/N falls between p<sub>i</sub>-[p<sub>i</sub>(1- p<sub>i</sub>)/N]<sup>1/2</sup> *1.96 and p<sub>i</sub>+[p<sub>i</sub>(1- p<sub>i</sub>)/N]<sup>1/2</sup> *1.96 that is designated by <span class=inline-formula><math display=inline id=M10 overflow=scroll><mrow><mtext>P</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo>-</mo><mn>1.96</mn><mo>*</mo><msqrt><mrow><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>-</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo and obeys </p><p class="" id=d5710e2375><a name=e5 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math5 overflow=scroll><mrow><mtext>P</mtext><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo>-</mo><mn>1.96</mn><mo>*</mo><msqrt><mrow><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo X<sub>i</sub>(N)/N, from which we want to find the confidence interval of p<sub>i </sub>that is the interval to which p<sub>i </sub>belongs with 95% certainty. If the observation provides the value X<sub>obs</sub> for X<sub>i</sub>(N) then, with 95% probability, the unknown quantity p<sub>i </sub>obeys</p><p class="" id=d5710e2511><a name=e6 id=d5710e2573>This inequality is equivalent to (X<sub>obs</sub> - p<sub>i</sub> N)<sup>2</sup> 1.96<sup>2</sup> p<sub>i</sub> N(1- p<sub>i</sub>), which gives</p><p class="" id=d5710e2595><a name=e8 stretchy=false>)</mo><mo></mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mo></mo><mn>0.</mn></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e2677>We find</p><p class="" id=d5710e2680><a name=e9 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math9 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>(X</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mtext>1</mtext><msup><mrow><mtext>.96</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>/2)</mtext><mo>/</mo><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo>+</mo><msup><mn>1.96</mn><mn>2</mn></msup><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>+</mo><mtext>Y</mtext><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e2778>where we defined </p><p class="" id=d5710e2781><a name=e10 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math10 overflow=scroll><mrow><mtext>Y</mtext><mo></mo><msqrt><mrow><msup><mtext>N</mtext><mn>2</mn></msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msup><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>(2X</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mtext>1</mtext><msup><mrow><mtext>.96</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>)</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mo></mo><mn>4</mn><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msup><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>(X</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mspace width="0.6em"></mspace><msup><mrow><mtext>(N</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mo>+</mo><mtext>1</mtext><msup><mrow><mtext>.96</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>N)</mtext></mrow></msqrt><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mtext>(N</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mo>+</mo><mtext>1</mtext><msup><mrow><mo>.96</mo></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mspace width="0.4em"></mspace><mtext>N)</mtext><mo>.</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e2888> We have neglected terms of order 1/N and introducing (pi)<sub>obs</sub> X<sub>obs</sub>/N that </p><p class="" id=d5710e2897><a name=e11 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math11 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>-</mo><mn>1.96</mn><mo>*</mo><msqrt><mrow><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>-</mo><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>/</mo><mtext>N</mtext></mrow></msqrt><mo></mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo></mo><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>1.96</mn><mo>*</mo><msqrt><mrow><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>-</mo><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>p</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mrow><mtext>obs</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>/</mo><mtext>N</mtext></mrow></msqrt><mo>,</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3041>with 95% probability. This has the same form as <a href="#e5">Equation (2)</a> above because p<sub>i </sub>and (p<sub>i</sub>)<sub>obs</sub> coincide in the leading order in N&gt;&gt;1. For not so large sample, however, there is a difference, the property which is often not mentioned in the discussions.</p><p class="" id=d5710e3056>In the rest of this work we keep using the definition of the confidence interval by 95% certainty. This and the factor of 1.96 above are somewhat arbitrary. If we used 90% confidence interval instead, then 1.645 would be present in the formula above instead of 1.96. This would result in more differences between the studied groups; however, we prefer to stick to the more conservative estimate of the differences.</p><p class="" id=d5710e3059><b><i>Data consistency with random sampling.</i></b> We have already reported that the assumption of perfectly random sampling is not accurate. However, our data is still composed of independent information units where the unit is defined by the information provided by one participant. This unit is the information on the close family of the participant. Hence for a large number of participants, considering with very good approximation the information that they provide as independent, the data is still the sum of independent identically distributed random variables. The variable is the number of people with profession i in one reported family. Therefore, by the central limit theorem (see e.g. <a href="#ref-6">Gnedenko &amp; Kolmogorov, 1968</a>), the distribution of the number of representatives of each profession is still Gaussian, as in the case of random sampling. Having the Gaussian distribution, we can then evaluate the confidence interval and obtain the appropriate generalization of the results of the previous section. Below we quantify these considerations and provide the changes that are in order.</p><p class="" id=d5710e3068><a href="#T3">Table 3</a> provides a typical excerpt from our list of individuals and their professions. It is seen that some surnames are included multiple times. In <a href="#T3">Table 3</a>, individuals with the same surname do not necessarily belong to the same family, as seen from the places of origin. In some cases (not shown), the bearers of the same names did belong to the same family and had similar professions. For instance in the list of 858 individuals with rabbinical surnames, we found two Berlins who were architects, two Wahls who were salesmen, two Halperins who were accountants, two Hellers who were teachers, three Hellers who were engineers, four Hellers who were economists, two Ginzburgs who were accountants and two who worked in delivery, two Gordovers who were engineers, two Gordons who were workers, and nine Horowitzs who were engineers. These correlations might cause the statistics to behave differently from the situation where each individual is picked at random from the studied group. This issue must be considered quantitatively. We observed previously that the number of reported people from the same family rarely exceeded five and typically was much less. For quantitative treatment of the effect of these correlations of limited range (below five), we consider the statistics of X<sub>i</sub>(N)  the number of individuals with profession i in the list with total number of individuals N. We introduce the random variable x<sub>i</sub>(p). This variable equals 1 if the individual in place p of the list has profession i and 0 otherwise. Then</p><p class="" id=d5710e3082><a name=e12 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math12 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>=</mo><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>p=1</mtext></mrow><mtext>N</mtext></msubsup><mrow></mrow></mstyle><msub><mtext>x</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>p</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>.</mo></mrow></math></div></p><a name=T3 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 3. These spellings were created in the course of family migrations during centuries because spellings of the same name in official documents differed in different countries, for example due to spelling mistakes in the records.</p></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" valign=top><a name=d5710e3394 class=n-a></a>Berlin</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e3397 class=n-a></a>Odessa, Ukraine</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e3409 class=n-a></a>Dzerzhinsk, Ukraine </td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e3412 class=n-a></a>Accountant</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d5710e3420>We assume that the list is ordered so that individuals with possible correlations of professions are grouped together; the list can be thought of as obtained in this way. We pick at random individuals from the considered group (bearers of rabbinical or occupational or generic surname) and then we pick their close family of random size as determined by the statistics of family sizes (which is of no interest here). This implies that x<sub>i</sub>(p) in the equation above have finite correlation range so that the variance &lt; x<sub>i</sub>(p) x<sub>i</sub>(p+k)&gt;- &lt; x<sub>i</sub>(p) &gt; &lt; x<sub>i</sub>(p+k)&gt; is non-zero for some positive integer k. k is bounded from above by k<sub>max</sub>, which is formally given by the maximal family size, which is fifteen (individual, parents, great and great great-parents); however, this is five in reality as we have already explained. For large N&gt;&gt;k<sub>max</sub> the distribution of X<sub>i</sub>(N) is still Gaussian as in the case of random sampling. This can be seen as the result of application of the central limit theorem to the sum of independent random variables where each variable is the sum of x<sub>i</sub>(p) over one family, i.e.</p><p class="" id=d5710e3451><a name=e13 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math13 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mo>=</mo><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mtext>l</mtext><mrow></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>y</mtext><mrow><mtext>family</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mtext>l</mtext></msub></mrow></mstyle><mo>,</mo><mspace width=2em></mspace><msub><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>y</mtext><mrow><mtext>family</mtext></mrow></msub><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mtext>l</mtext></msub><mo>=</mo><mstyle displaystyle=true><msub><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>l-th family</mtext></mrow></msub><mrow><msub><mtext>x</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(p)</mtext><mo>.</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3528>Here we introduced the random variable (y<sub>family</sub>)<sub>l </sub>, which counts the number of representatives of profession i in the l-th family where l is the index of the family. Thus the statistics of (y<sub>family</sub>)<sub>l </sub>could be obtained by partitioning the considered group of population (rabbinical, occupational or generic) into families, where the family is defined as information unit in our data and not otherwise, with the units definition given in the beginning of this section. Considering (y<sub>family</sub>)<sub>l </sub>with different l as independent, we conclude from the central limit theorem that X<sub>i</sub>(N) is approximately Gaussian random variable, which is uniquely characterized by its mean and variance (a finer consideration can be done using the version of the central limit theorem for random variables with fast decaying correlations, see Gnedenko and Kolmogorov). These are given by</p><p class="" id=d5710e3553><a name=e14 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math14 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt; X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(N)</mtext><mspace width="0.5em"></mspace><mo>&gt;=</mo><mtext>p</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msub><mrow><mtext>N</mtext><mo>,</mo><mspace width="1.5em"></mspace><mo>&lt;</mo><mtext>(X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(N)-&lt;X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(N)</mtext><mspace width="0.5em"></mspace><mo>&gt;</mo><mtext>)</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>&gt;= </mtext><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>p=1</mtext></mrow><mtext>N</mtext></msubsup><mrow><mspace width="0.5em"></mspace><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>k=1</mtext></mrow><mtext>N</mtext></msubsup><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>(&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p)x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(k)&gt;- &lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p)</mtext><mo> &gt;</mo><mspace width="0.5em"></mspace><mo>&lt;x</mo></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(k)&gt;)</mtext></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mstyle><mspace width=8em></mspace><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mn>3</mn><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3670>where we used directly the definition X<sub>i</sub>(N)= <sup>N</sup><sub>p=1</sub> x<sub>i</sub>(p). Here &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p)&gt;= p<sub>i</sub>, where p<sub>i</sub> were defined in the previous section. We can consider the last sum as sum of diagonal and off-diagonal elements. The sum over the diagonal elements is readily found by using that x<sub>i</sub>(p)=1 with probability p<sub>i</sub> and x<sub>i</sub>(p)=0 with probability 1-p<sub>i</sub>. Thus &lt;x<sup>2</sup><sub>i</sub>(p)&gt;=p<sub>i</sub> and</p><p class="" id=d5710e3715><a name=e15 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math15 overflow=scroll><mrow><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>p=1</mtext></mrow><mtext>N</mtext></msubsup><mrow><msubsup><mrow><mtext>(&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msubsup><msub><mrow><mtext>(p)&gt; -&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(p)&gt;</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><msub><mrow><mtext>)=p</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msub><mrow><mtext>N(1-p</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>),</mtext></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3767>which is the same answer as for the random sampling. It is the sum of the off-diagonal terms <sub>pk</sub> (&lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p) x<sub>i</sub>(k)&gt;- &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p) &gt; &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(k)&gt;), a non-trivial quantity, which characterizes the correlations of professions within one family. Thus &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p) x<sub>i</sub>(p+1)&gt; is the probability that two consecutive individuals from the list have identical profession i. This probability is larger than &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p)&gt; &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p+1)&gt;, which would hold if professions of the individuals were independent. The finite difference &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p) x<sub>i</sub>(p+1)&gt;- &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p)&gt; &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p+1)&gt; is caused by the finite probability that individuals p and p+1 belong to the same family and thus have positively correlated professions. It seems inevitable that within-family correlations are positive so that</p><p class="" id=d5710e3811><a name=e16 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math16 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p) x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p+k)&gt; - &lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p)&gt;&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(k)&gt;</mtext><mo></mo><mtext>0</mtext><mo>.</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3849>The left-hand side of the above equation is identically zero for k&gt; k<sub>max</sub> (see above). It is then readily seen from the definition in <a href="#e14">Equation (3)</a> above that the variance obeys</p><p class="" id=d5710e3858><a name=e17 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math17 overflow=scroll><mrow><mo>&lt;</mo><msup><mrow><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext> - &lt;</mtext><mi> </mi><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mi> </mi><mo>&gt;</mo><mo stretchy=false>)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><mtext>&gt;=</mtext><mi> </mi><mtext>&lt;(</mtext><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(N))</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><mi> </mi><mo>-</mo><mi> </mi><mo>&lt;</mo><mi> </mi><msub><mtext>X</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mo stretchy=false>(</mo><mtext>N</mtext><mo stretchy=false>)</mo><mi> </mi><msup><mo>&gt;</mo><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>=</mo><mi> </mi><msub><mtext>c</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.5em"></mspace><mtext>N</mtext><mspace width="1.5em"></mspace><mtext>N</mtext><mo>&gt;&gt;</mo><msub><mtext>k</mtext><mrow><mtext>max</mtext></mrow></msub></mrow></math> </div></p><p class="" id=d5710e3979>where the constant c<sub>i</sub> is independent of N. The question is how different c<sub>i</sub> is from the random sampling value p<sub>i</sub>(1-p<sub>i</sub>) because of the described correlations.</p><p class="" id=d5710e3994>We face the problem of estimating the normalized dispersion c<sub>i</sub> for our unknown sampling statistics. This can be done quite accurately in the case of bearers of generic surnames where we have data on more than 7400 individuals. This is accomplished by partitioning the list into 74 hundreds and considering the corresponding 74 numbers [X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k</sup> with k running from 1 to 74 as independent realizations of X<sub>i</sub>(100). Here the independence of [X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k</sup> with different k holds due to 100&gt;&gt;k<sub>max</sub>, which implies that the number of correlated professions in different hundreds is negligibly small in comparison with the total numbers. Indeed, we have</p><p class="" id=d5710e4020><a name=e18 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math18 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt;[X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(100)]</mtext><msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>k</mtext></msup><msub><mrow><mtext>[X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(100)]</mtext><msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mrow><mtext>k+1</mtext></mrow></msup><mtext>&gt;=&lt;(</mtext><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>p=1</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>100</mtext></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msub><mtext>x</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(p)) (</mtext><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>k=101</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>200</mtext></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msub><mtext>x</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(k))&gt; =</mtext><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>p=1</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>100</mtext></mrow></msubsup><mrow><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>k=101</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>200</mtext></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt;x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msub><mrow><mtext>(p)x</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(k)&gt;</mtext></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e4125>where &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p)(x<sub>i</sub>(k)&gt; differs from &lt;x<sub>i</sub>(p)&gt;&lt;x<sub>i</sub>(k)&gt; only for indices p and k in narrow vicinity of p=k=100, which can be neglected. We find the independent variables law &lt;[X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k</sup>[X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k+1</sup>&gt;=&lt;[X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k</sup>&gt;&lt;[X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k+1</sup>&gt; (similar consideration can be done for higher order correlations). This independence is the reason why we group the data in blocks of 100 (of course the block size is not defined uniquely). We observe that Y<sub>i</sub>(1/74) <sup>74</sup><sub>k=1</sub>([X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup> k</sup>)<sup>2 </sup>is a sum of independent Gaussian variables and hence it is Gaussian itself. The average of Y<sub>i</sub> is &lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(100))<sup>2</sup>&gt; and its dispersion is</p><p class="" id=d5710e4193><a name=e19 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math19 overflow=scroll><mrow><msubsup><mrow><mtext>&lt;Y</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msubsup><msub><mrow><mtext>&gt; - &lt;Y</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msup><msup><mrow><mtext>=(1/74</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>)</mtext><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mstyle displaystyle=true><msubsup><mo></mo><mrow><mtext>k=1</mtext></mrow><mrow><mtext>74</mtext></mrow></msubsup><mrow><msub><mrow><mtext>(&lt;([X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>(100)]</mtext><msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>k</mtext></msup><msup><mtext>)</mtext><mtext>4</mtext></msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mtext>-</mtext></msup><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt;([X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(100)]</mtext></mrow><mtext>k</mtext></msup><msup><mtext>)</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msup><msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>)</mtext><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msub><mrow><mtext>=(1/37) &lt;(X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(100)])</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><mtext>2</mtext></msup></mrow></mstyle><mo>.</mo></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e4307>where we used independence of [X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup> k</sup> with different k and that Gaussianity of [X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup> k</sup> implies &lt;([X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup> k</sup>)<sup>4</sup>&gt;=3 &lt;([X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup> k</sup>)<sup>2</sup>&gt;<sup>2</sup>=3 &lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(100)])<sup>2</sup>&gt;<sup>2</sup>. Thus the distribution of (Y<sub>i</sub>/&lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(100))<sup>2</sup>&gt;-1) 37 is the standard normal distribution with zero mean and unit variance. We find that the value of Y<sub>i</sub> obtained from our data limits &lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(100))<sup>2</sup>&gt; within the interval given by</p><p class="" id=d5710e4373><a name=e20 class=n-a></a><div class="disp-formula panel"><math display=block id=math20 overflow=scroll><mrow><msub><mtext>Y</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><mo>-1</mo><msub><mrow><mtext>.96Y</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>/</mtext><msqrt><mrow><mn>37</mn></mrow></msqrt><msub><mrow><mtext>&lt;&lt;(X</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><msup><mrow><mtext>(100))</mtext></mrow><mtext>2</mtext></msup><mtext>&gt;</mtext><mspace width="0.8em"></mspace><mtext>&lt;</mtext><mspace width="0.2em"></mspace><msub><mtext>Y</mtext><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>+1</mtext><msub><mrow><mtext>.96 Y</mtext></mrow><mtext>i</mtext></msub><mtext>/</mtext><msqrt><mrow><mn>37</mn></mrow></msqrt></mrow></math></div></p><p class="" id=d5710e4437>with the confidence level of 95% (see above). The random sampling would give &lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(N))<sup>2</sup>&gt;= p<sub>i </sub>N+N(N-1) p<sub>i</sub><sup>2</sup> with N=100 as seen readily from the properties of the binomial distribution. The comparison between the dispersion determined from our data and the dispersion predicted by the random sampling assumption is provided in <a href="#T4">Table 4</a>. </p><a name=T4 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 4. Comparison of dispersion evaluated from the sample data (second column) and the random sampling prediction (third column) for generic surnames.</h3><p id=d5710e4468>The agreement is found to be much narrower than allowed by the confidence interval provided in the fourth and fifth columns.</p></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d5710e4472 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d5710e4474 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d5710e4476 class=n-a></a><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e4478 class=n-a></a>Occupations</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e4481 class=n-a></a>Observed &lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(100))<sup>2</sup>&gt;;<br class=br><sup>74</sup><sub>k=1</sub>([X<sub>i</sub>(100)]<sup>k</sup>)<sup>2</sup>/74</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e4507 class=n-a></a>Random sampling;<br class=br>&lt;(X<sub>i</sub>(N))<sup>2</sup>&gt;=100 p<sub>i</sub> +9900p<sub>i</sub><sup>2</sup></th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e4525 class=n-a></a>Lower end of<br class=br>confidence interval</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d5710e4530 class=n-a></a>Upper end of<br class=br>confidence interval</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d5710e4537 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d5710e4539 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d5710e4541 class=n-a></a>1.
